 </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n          <a href="#cite_ref-Lazard_10-4">\n           <sup>\n            <i>\n             <b>\n              e\n             </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          Lazard, Gilbert 1975, "The Rise of the New Persian Language" in Frye, R. N.,\n          <i>\n           The Cambridge History of Iran\n          </i>\n          , Vol. 4, pp. 595\xe2\x80\x93632, Cambridge: Cambridge University Press. "The language known as New Persian, which usually is called at this period (early Islamic times) by the name of Dari or Farsi-Dari, can be classified linguistically as a continuation of Middle Persian, the official religious and literary language of Sassanian Iran, itself a continuation of Old Persian, the language of the Achaemenids. Unlike the other languages and dialects, ancient and modern, of the Iranian group such as\n          <a href="/wiki/Avestan" title="Avestan">\n           Avestan\n          </a>\n          ,\n          <a href="/wiki/Parthian_language" title="Parthian language">\n           Parthian\n          </a>\n          ,\n          <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_language" title="Sogdian language">\n           Soghdian\n          </a>\n          ,\n          <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">\n           Kurdish\n          </a>\n          ,\n          <a href="/wiki/Balochi_language" title="Balochi language">\n           Balochi\n          </a>\n          ,\n          <a href="/wiki/Pashto" title="Pashto">\n           Pashto\n          </a>\n          , etc.,\n          <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">\n           Old Persian\n          </a>\n          ,\n          <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">\n           Middle\n          </a>\n          and New Persian represent one and the same language at three states of its history. It had its origin in\n          <a href="/wiki/Fars_Province" title="Fars Province">\n           Fars\n          </a>\n          (the true Persian country from the historical point of view) and is differentiated by dialectical features, still easily recognizable from the dialect prevailing in north-western and eastern Iran."\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-11">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-11">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          Ulrich Ammon, Norbert Dittmar, Klaus J. Mattheier, Peter Trudgill, "Sociolinguistics Hsk 3/3 Series Volume 3 of Sociolinguistics: An International Handbook of the Science of Language and Society", Walter de Gruyter, 2006. 2nd edition. pg 1912. Excerpt: "Middle Persian, also called Pahlavi is a direct continuation of old Persian, and was used as the written official language of the country." "However, after the Moslem conquest and the collapse of the\n          <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">\n           Sassanids\n          </a>\n          , the\n          <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">\n           Pahlavi language\n          </a>\n          was gradually replaced by Dari, a variety of Middle Persian, with considerable loan elements from Arabic and Parthian."\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-12">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-12">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          Skj\xc3\xa6rv\xc3\xb8, Prods Oktor (2006).\n          <i>\n           Encyclopedia Iranica\n          </i>\n          , "Iran, vi. Iranian languages and scripts, "new Persian, is "the descendant of Middle Persian" and has since been "official language of Iranian states for centuries", whereas for other non-Persian Iranian languages "close genetic relationships are difficult to establish" between their different (Middle and Modern) stages. Modern Ya\xe1\xb8\xa1n\xc5\x8dbi belongs to the same dialect group as Sogdian, but is not a direct descendant; Bactrian may be closely related to modern Yid\xe1\xb8\xa1a and Munji (Munj\xc4\x81ni); and\n          <a href="/wiki/Wakhi_language" title="Wakhi language">\n           Wakhi\n          </a>\n          (W\xc4\x81\xe1\xb8\xb5i) belongs with Khotanese."\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-Richard_Davis_2006._pp._602-603-13">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          ^\n          <a href="#cite_ref-Richard_Davis_2006._pp._602-603_13-0">\n           <sup>\n            <i>\n             <b>\n              a\n             </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n          <a href="#cite_ref-Richard_Davis_2006._pp._602-603_13-1">\n           <sup>\n            <i>\n             <b>\n              b\n             </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n          <a href="#cite_ref-Richard_Davis_2006._pp._602-603_13-2">\n           <sup>\n            <i>\n             <b>\n              c\n             </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          Richard Davis, "Persian" in Josef W. Meri, Jere L. Bacharach, "Medieval Islamic Civilization", Taylor &amp; Francis, 2006. pp. 602\xe2\x80\x93603. "The grammar of New Persian is similar to many contemporary European languages."Similarly, the core vocabulary of Persian continued to be derived from Pahlavi.\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-14">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-14">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation web">\n           <a class="external text" href="http://parents.berkeley.edu/madar-pedar/Persian_or_Farsi.html" rel="nofollow">\n            "Persian or Farsi?"\n           </a>\n           .\n           <i>\n            parents.berkeley.edu\n           </i>\n           <span class="reference-accessdate">\n            . Excerpt 1: "Only the official languages Old, Middle, and New Persian represent three stages of one and the same language, whereas close genetic relationships are difficult to establish between other Middle and Modern Iranian languages. Modern Ya\xe1\xb8\xa1n\xc5\x8dbi belongs to the same dialect group as Sogdian, but is not a direct descendant; Bactrian may be closely related to modern Yid\xe1\xb8\xa1a and Munji (Munj\xc4\x81ni); and Wakhi (W\xc4\x81\xe1\xb8\xb5i) belongs with Khotanese.\n          <i>\n           Excerpt 2:\n          </i>\n          New Persian, the descendant of Middle Persian and official language of Iranian states for centuries."\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-s2008-80-1-45">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a Kent, Old Persian, 1953\n          </a>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-OPGTL_6-50">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-OPGTL_6_50-0">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          Kent, R. Documentation. Old Persian.)\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-52">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-52">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation book">\n           <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">\n            Xenophon\n           </a>\n           .\n           <i>\n            <a href="/wiki/Anabasis_(Xenophon)" title="Anabasis (Xenophon)">\n             Anabasis\n            </a>\n           </i>\n           . pp.\xc2\xa0IV.v.2\xe2\x80\x939.\n          </cite>\n          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Anabasis&amp;rft.pages=IV.v.2-9&amp;rft.au=Xenophon&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APersian+language">\n           <span style="display:none;">\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-53">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-53">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFComrie2003">\n           Comrie, Bernard (2003).\n           <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=5IXJ1RbE8-8C" rel="nofollow">\n            <i>\n             The Major Languages of South Asia, the Middle East and Africa\n            </i>\n           </a>\n           . Routledge.\n           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">\n            ISBN\n           </a>\n           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-134-93257-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-134-93257-3">\n            978-1-134-93257-3\n           </a>\n           .\n          </cite>\n          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Major+Languages+of+South+Asia%2C+the+Middle+East+and+Africa&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-134-93257-3&amp;rft.aulast=Comrie&amp;rft.aufirst=Bernard&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D5IXJ1RbE8-8C&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APersian+language">\n           <span style="display:none;">\n           </span>\n          </span>\n          , p. 82. "The evolution of Persian as the culturally dominant language of major parts of the Near East, from Anatolia and Iran, to Central Asia, to northwest India until recent centuries, began with the political domination of these areas by dynasties originating in southwestern province of Iran, Pars, later Arabicised to Fars: first the Achaemenids (599\xe2\x80\x93331 BC) whose official language was Old Persian; then the Sassanids (c. AD 225\xe2\x80\x93651) whose official language was Middle Persian. Hence, the entire country used to be called Perse by the ancient Greeks, a practice continued to this day. The more general designation \'Iran(-shahr)" derives from Old Iranian aryanam (Khshathra)\' (the realm) of Aryans\'. The dominance of these two dynasties resulted in Old and Middle-Persian colonies throughout the empire, most importantly for the course of the development of Persian, in the north-east i.e., what is now Khorasan, northern Afghanistan and Central Asia, as documented by the Middle Persian texts of the Manichean found in the oasis city of Turfan in Chinese Turkistan (\n          <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">\n           Sinkiang\n          </a>\n          ). This led to certain degree of regionalisation".\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-54">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-54">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          Comrie, Bernard (1990)\n          <i>\n           The major languages of South Asia, the Middle East and Africa\n          </i>\n          , Taylor &amp; Francis, p. 82\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-55">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-55">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          Barbara M. Horvath, Paul Vaughan,\n          <i>\n           Community languages\n          </i>\n          , 1991, p. 276\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-56">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-56">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          L. Paul (2005), "The Language of the Shahnameh in historical and dialectical perspective", p. 150: "The language of the Shahnameh should be seen as one instance of continuous historical development from Middle to New Persian.",\n          <i>\n           in\n          </i>\n          <cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFWeberMacKenzie2005">\n           Weber, Dieter; MacKenzie, D. Williams.pp.17\xe2\x80\x9319.\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-Adamec-61">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-Adamec_61-0">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation book">\n           Adamec, Ludwig W. (2011).\n           <i>\n            Historical Dictionary of Afghanistan\n           </i>\n           (4th Revised ed.). Scarecrow. p.\xc2\xa0105.\n           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">\n            ISBN\n           </a>\n           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8108-7815-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8108-7815-0">\n            978-0-8108-7815-0\n           </a>\n           .\n          </cite>\n          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Historical+Dictionary+of+Afghanistan&amp;rft.pages=105&amp;rft.edition=4th+Revised&amp;rft.pub=Scarecrow&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8108-7815-0&amp;rft.aulast=Adamec&amp;rft.aufirst=Ludwig+W.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APersian+language">\n           <span style="display:none;">\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-62">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-62">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          according to\n          <a class="external text" href="http://www.iranchamber.com/literature/articles/persian_language.php" rel="nofollow">\n           iranchamber.com\n          </a>\n          "the language (ninth to thirteenth centuries), preserved in the literature of the Empire, is known as Classical Persian, due to the eminence and distinction of poets such as Roudaki, Ferdowsi, and Khayyam. During this period, Persian was adopted as the lingua franca of the eastern Islamic nations. Extensive contact with Arabic led to a large influx of Arab vocabulary. In fact, a writer of Classical Persian had at one\'s disposal the entire Arabic lexicon and could use Arab terms freely either for literary effect or to display erudition. Classical Persian remained essentially unchanged until the nineteenth century, when the dialect of Teheran rose in prominence, having been chosen as the capital of Persia by the Qajar Dynasty in 1787. This Modern Persian dialect became the basis of what is now called Contemporary Standard Persian. Although it still contains a large number of Arab terms, most borrowings have been nativized, with a much lower percentage of Arabic words in colloquial forms of the language."\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-63">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-63">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          John Andrew Boyle,\n          <i>\n           Some thoughts on the sources for the Il-Khanid period of Persian history\n          </i>\n          , in\n          <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">\n           Iran\n          </a>\n          : Journal of the British Institute of Persian Studies, British Institute of Persian Studies, vol. 12 (1974), p. 175.\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-Sigfried_J_1994._p_734-64">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          ^\n          <a href="#cite_ref-Sigfried_J_1994._p_734_64-0">\n           <sup>\n            <i>\n             <b>\n              a\n             </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n          <a href="#cite_ref-Sigfried_J_1994._p_734_64-1">\n           <sup>\n            <i>\n             <b>\n              b\n             </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFde_Laet1994">\n           de Laet, Sigfried J. "The Turks, on the other hand, posed a formidable threat: their penetration into Iranian lands was considerable, to such an extent that vast regions adapted their language. This process was all the more remarkable since, in spite of their almost uninterrupted political domination for nearly 1,500 years, the cultural influence of these rough nomads on Iran\'s refined civilization remained extremely tenuous. This is demonstrated by the mediocre linguistic contribution, for which exhaustive statistical studies have been made (Doerfer). The number of Turkish or Mongol words that entered Persian, though not negligible, remained limited to 2,135, i.e., 3 percent of the vocabulary at the most. These new words are confined on the one hand to the military and political sector (titles, administration, etc.) and, on the other hand, to technical pastoral terms. The contrast with Arab influence is striking. While cultural pressure of the Arabs on Iran had been intense, they in no way infringed upon the entire Iranian territory, whereas with the Turks, whose contributions to Iranian civilization were modest, vast regions of Iranian lands were assimilated, notwithstanding the fact that resistance by the latter was ultimately victorious. Several reasons may be offered."\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-87">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-87">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation web">\n           <a class="external text" href="http://www.iranicaonline.org/articles/armenia-iv" rel="nofollow">\n            "ARMENIA AND IRAN iv. Iranian influences in Armenian Language"\n           </a>\n           <span class="reference-accessdate">\n            .
